   <table cellpadding="4" width="100%">
    <tr>
     <td bgcolor="#336699">
      <font color="WHITE" size="2">
       <b>
        Addams, Jane
       </b>
       [1860-1935]
      </font>
     </td>
    </tr>
   </table>
   <p>
    <img align="right" alt="Jane Addams" border="0" hspace="30" src="../images/sociologists/jane_addams.jpg"/>
    <font face="Comic Sans MS" size="2">
     Jane Addams was one of the vice presidents of the Chicago Liberty Meeting that led to the formation of the Central Anti-Imperialist League in Chicago. She later served as a vice president of the national Anti-Imperialist League (1904-1919). In 1889, Addams co-founded Hull House, one of Americas first settlement houses. She was later a co-founder and first president of the Womens International League for Peace and Freedom (1919-1935). She is also remembered as the first American Woman to receive the Nobel Peace Price.
    </font>
   </p>
   <ul>
    <li>
     <b>
      Biography
     </b>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="http://media.pfeiffer.edu/lridener/DSS/#addams">
        Biography
       </a>
       <br/>
       From: Marshall W. Fischwick [1968]
       <i>
        Illustrious American: Jane Adams
       </i>
       . Morristown, NJ: Silver Burdett Company.
       <br/>
       From:  Mary Jo Deegan [1985]
       <i>
        Jane Addams and the Men of the Chicago School, 1892-1018
       </i>
       . New Brunswick NJ: Transaction Books.
      </li>
      <li>

       </a>

      At the age of twelve Jane Addams learned from her father that international patriotism is not a contradictory expression. She obtained a sense of the genuine relationship which may exist between men who share large hopes and like desires, even though they differ in nationality, language and creed.

      <font color="WHITE" size="2">
       <b>
        Adorno, Theodor W.
       </b>
       [1903 - 1969]
      </font>
     </td>
    </tr>
   </table>
   <p>
    <b>
     By Adorno
    </b>
   </p>
   <ul>
    <img align="right" alt="Theodor W. Adorno" src="../images/sociologists/adorno.jpg"/>
    <li>
     [1930/1964]
     <a href="http://muse.jhu.edu/journals/yale_journal_of_criticism/v009/9.1adorno.html">
      Chaplin Times Two
     </a>
     <br/>
     The first essay appeared under the title Kierkegaard prophesies Chaplin in the
     <i>
      Frankfurter Zeitung,
     </i>
     May 22, 1930; the second as Chaplin in Malibu in
     <i>
      Neue Rundschau,
     </i>
     Vol. 3, 75th year, 1964; they first appeared together under the present title in the volume
     <i>
      Ohne Leitbild
     </i>
     (Frankfurt am Main: Suhrkamp, 1969), 89-93.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1944]
     <a href="http://www.academia.edu/8504692/Le_Prix_du_Progr%C3%A8s">
      Le Prix du Progrs
     </a>
     (with Max Horkheimer)
     <br/>
     From: The Dialectic of Enlightenment. New York: Continuum, 1993. Originally published as Dialektik der Aufklrung, 1944.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1944]
     <a href="http://www.marxists.org/reference/archive/adorno/1944/culture-industry.htm">
      The Culture Industry: Enlightment as Mass Deception
     </a>
     (with
     <a href="#HORKHEIMER" target="_self">
      Max Horkheimer
     </a>
     )
     <br/>
     From: The Dialectic of Enlightenment. New York: Continuum, 1993. Originally published as Dialektik der Aufklrung, 1944.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1951]
     <a href="http://www.efn.org/~dredmond/MinimaMoralia.html">
      Minima moralia. Reflections from damaged life
     </a>
     [
     <a href="http://www.marxists.org/reference/archive/adorno/1951/mm/index.htm">
      mirror
     </a>
     ] (First published in German in 1951).  Translation: Dennis Redmond (2005)
     <br/>
     Dialectic thought is an attempt to break through the coercion of logic by its own means.
     <br/>
     <a href="http://platypus1917.home.comcast.net/~platypus1917/adorno_imaginativeexcesses.pdf">
      Imaginative excesses
     </a>
     (unpublished piece intended for
     <i>
      Minima Moralia
     </i>
     ).
.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1956]
     <a href="http://www.msu.edu/~sullivan/AdornoMusLangFrag.html">
      Music and Language: A Fragment
     </a>
     <br/>
     From: Quasi una Fantasia. Essays on Modern Music. London/New York: Verso.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1964]
     <a href="http://www.kritiknetz.de/index.php/kritischetheorie/154-jargondereigentlichkeitzurdeutschenideologie">
      Jargon der Eigentlichkeit. Zur deutschen Ideologie
     </a>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1966]
     <a href="http://www.heathwoodpress.com/wp-content/uploads/2014/05/AdornoEducation.pdf">
      Education After Auschwitz
     </a>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1968]
     <a href="http://www.marxists.org/reference/archive/adorno/1968/late-capitalism.htm">
      Late Capitalism or Industrial Society?
     </a>
     <br/>
     Opening Adress to the 16th German Sociological Congress. Translation: Dennis Redmond (2001)
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1970/2001]
     <a href="http://members.efn.org/~dredmond/ndtrans.html">
      Negative Dialectics
     </a>
     |
     <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Negative_Dialectics">
      Wikipedia
     </a>
     <br/>
     Translated by Dennis Redmond.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1991]
     <a href="texts/adorno_culture_reconsidered.pdf">
      Culture Industry Reconsidered
     </a>
     <br/>
     From: The Culture Industry: Selected Essays on Mass Culture. London: Routledge, 1991.
    </li>
   </ul>

      About Beckett and the deformed subject
     </a>
     [0:36]
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      Adorno speaks in a TV talk show about the plays of Beckett and the necessary reduction of the plays because of blunted human beings and deformed subjects. Translated in English he says: Everyone says Beckett is a reduction of tecnique to the extreme, I said that myself and it is nothing special about it to say that. But this reduction is what the world makes out of us, to talk with Karl Kraus. This is what the world is doing to us. These human stubs. These humans that lost their I - they are the products of the world they live in.
     </span>

     Larsen, Neil [1992]
     <br/>
     <a href="http://pmc.iath.virginia.edu/text-only/issue.192/larsen.192">
      Metadorno
     </a>
     <br/>
     In: Postmodern Culture 2(2).
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     Lindsey, Jack [1981]
     <br/>
     <a href="http://www.autodidactproject.org/other/lindsay6-adorno.html">
      Adornohttp://www.autodidactproject.org/other/lindsay6-adorno.html and the Frankfurt School
     </a>
     <br/>
     In: Lindsay, Jack [1981] The Crisis in Marxism (Totowa, NJ: Barnes &amp; Noble Books, 1981), Chapter 5, Adorno and the Frankfurt School, pp. 63-80, 164-166.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     OBrien, Eugene [1968]
     <br/>
     <a href="http://www.ul.ie/~philos/vol2/negation.html">
      What ish my nation?: Towards a negative definition of identity
     </a>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     Welty, Gordon [1984]
     <br/>
     <a href="http://www.wright.edu/~gordon.welty/Adorno_84.htm">
      Theodor Adorno and the Culture Industry
     </a>
     <br/>
     Presented at the Annual Meeting of the Popular Culture Association, Toronto (March 30, 1984)
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      A review of Adornos conception of the culture industry as it is found in three writings: On Popular Music (1941), Dialectic of Enlightenment (1944), and Culture Industry Reconsidered (1963). In these writings, Adorno has provided a theory of the nature of the culture product and its valuation. The standardization and interchangeability of culture products under late capitalism leads to the interchangeability of persons in the audience. Stylization has its counterpart, the pseudo-individualization of the culture product and members of the audience. Both stylization and pseudo-individualization contribute to the possibilities of mass marketing. The consequences for the audience in late capitalism are distraction on the one hand, and on the other hand, a means of ensuring the audiences adjustment to its dependency. The significance of Adornos thought on the culture industry is becoming increasingly apparent in the present era.
     </span>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     Wiggersfhaus, Rolf [1995]
     <br/>
     <a href="http://books.google.nl/books/about/The_Frankfurt_School.html?id=vZMjjW4uOWIC">
      The Frankfurt School: Its History, Theories, and Political Significance
     </a>
     (Translated by: Michael Robertson).
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theodor_Adorno">
      Wikipedia: Theodor Adorno
     </a>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     Williams, Howard [1989]
     <br/>
     <a href="http://www.autodidactproject.org/other/adorno-wms.html">
      Adornos Negative Dialectic
     </a>
     <br/>
     In: Williams, Howard [1989] Hegel, Heraclitus, and Marxs Dialectic (New York: St. Martins Press, 1989), Chapter 9, Conclusion, pp. 220-223, 241
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>

     <br/>

     </a>

     In: Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy
    </li>

     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      Bauman gives a lecture
      <i>
       Selves as Objects of Consumption
      </i>
      in the Tolerance Center of Vilnius Gaon Jewish State Museum. The lecture was organized by Lithuanian Member of the European Parliament Prof. Leonidas Donskis, publishing house
      <i>
       Apostrofa
      </i>
      and Vytautas Magnus University (Kaunas, Lithuania). The
      <a href="http://transcripts-with-permission-nz01.blogspot.nl/2011/07/selves-as-objects-of-consumption_02.html">
       transcript
      </a>
      of this lecture was created by James C.
     </span>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [2011]
     <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=DfkcTVzTEsk">
      The Global Factory of Wasted Humans, part 1
     </a>
     [13:01],
     <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8FERsOMlpUE&amp;feature=related">
      2
     </a>
     [10:05],
     <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=m_m8LTJqO8M&amp;feature=related">
      3
     </a>
     [13:45]
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      Bauman talks about
      <i>
       The Global Factory of Wasted Humans
      </i>
      in a filmed conference published on the French video portal Audiovisual Research Archive. For Bauman the production of human waste  or more precisely, wasted lives, the superfluous populations of migrants, refugees and other outcasts  is an inevitable outcome of modernization. It is an unavoidable side-effect of economic progress and the quest for order which is characteristic of modernity. As long as large parts of the world remained wholly or partly unaffected by modernization, they were treated by modernizing societies as lands that were able to absorb the excess of population in the developed countries. Global solutions were sought, and temporarily found, to locally produced overpopulation problems. But as modernization has reached the furthest lands of the planet, redundant population is produced everywhere and all localities have to bear the consequences of modernitys global triumph. They are now confronted with the need to seek local solutions to globally produced problems. The global spread of the modernity has given rise to growing quantities of human beings who are deprived of adequate means of survival, but the planet is fast running out of places to put them. Hence the new anxieties about immigrants and asylum seekers and the growing role played by diffuse security fears on the contemporary political agenda. See his book
      <i>
       Wasted Lives: Modernity and Its Outcasts
      </i>
      [2004].
     </span>
    </li>
   </ul>

    <p>
    </p>
    <li>
     <a href="http://specialcollections.vassar.edu/findingaids/benedict_ruth.html">
      Guide to the Ruth Fulton Benedict Papers
     </a>
     <p>

     [1931]
     <a href="http://sacred-texts.com/nam/sw/tci/index.htm">

    <li>
     [1950]
     <a href="http://classiques.uqac.ca/classiques/Benedicth_ruth/benedict.html">
      chantillons de civilisation
     </a>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     Hochman, Susan K.:
     <a href="http://www2.webster.edu/~woolflm/ruthbenedict.html">
      Ruth Fulton Benedict
     </a>
     <br/>
     A short but informative biography. You will learn that Benedict emphasizes the power of custom and learning as an argument against nature  and of the infinite capacity of human beings to change. Benedict believed that an individual could succesfully alter the conditions of her social life and in doing so, transforms society.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     Mead, Margaret [1949]
     <a href="http://www.americanethnography.com/article.php?id=7">
      Ruth Benedicts obituary
     </a>
     <br/>
     In: American Anthropologist 51(3):457-468.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ruth_Fulton_Benedict">
      Wikipedia: Ruth_Fulton_Benedict
     </a>

   <p>
    <a name="BERGER">
    </a>
   </p>
   <table cellpadding="4" width="100%">
    <tr>
     <td bgcolor="#336699">
      <a href="#top" target="_self">
       <img align="right" alt="Index" border="0" src="../images/up.gif"/>
      </a>
      <font color="WHITE" size="2">
       <b>
        Berger, Peter L. and Luckmann, Thomas
       </b>
      </font>
     </td>
    </tr>
   </table>
   <ul>
    <li type="square">
     [1963]
     <b>
      Invitation to Sociology. A Humanistic Perspective
     </b>
     <br/>
     New York, Anchor Books, Doubley &amp; Company, Inc.
     <br/>
     An invitation to understand society in a disciplined way for people who are naturally interested in the events that engage peoples ultimate beliefs, their moments of tragedy and grandeur and ecstasy, but who are also fascinated by the commonplace, the everyday.  Social reality has many layers of meaning. The discovery of each new layer changes the perceptions of the whole The fascination of sociology lies in the fact that its perspective makes us see in a new light the very world in which we have lived all our lives.
     <ul>
      <li type="square">
       <a href="texts/berger.pdf">
        Chapter 1: Sociology as an individual pastime
       </a>
       <br/>
       In the first chapter [pp. 1-24] of his
       <i>
        Invitation to Sociology
       </i>
       Berger presents his humanistic answers to the question why study sociology? In
       <a href="http://public.csusm.edu/public/brigham/Why_study_sociology.htm">
        Why Study Sociology?
       </a>
       his main arguments are -gender modified- summarized.
       <p>
       </p>
      </li>
      <li type="square">
       <a href="texts/berger_invitation_6.php">
        Chapter 6: Sociological Perspective: Society as Drama
       </a>
       <p>
       </p>
      </li>
      <li type="square">
       [1963]
       <a href="http://www.angelfire.com/or/sociologyshop/ITSOUT.html">
        Outline of
        <i>
         Invitation to Sociology
        </i>
       </a>
       <br/>
       An excellent introduction to sociology. Although some material is unavoidably dated, the sociological insights arent dated in the least.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1966]
     <a href="texts/berger_luckman.php">
      Society as a Human Product
     </a>
     <br/>
     From: Peter L. Berger and Thomas Luckmann. [1966] The Social Construction of Reality: A Treatise in the Sociology of Knowledge. Garden City, New York: Anchor Books,  pp. 51-55, 59-61.
     <br/>
     In these fragments the autors explain what it means that man produces himself, i.e.
     <i>
      the homo socius
     </i>
     . They also argue that the causes of the emergence, maintenance and transmission of a social order can only be understood when you analyse the orgins, functions and processes of institutionalization. The origin of institutionalization is explained by the argument that all human activity is subject to habitualization. This processes of baibualization precede any institutionalization. Institutionalization itself is defined as a reciprocal typication of habitualized actions by types of actors.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1990]
     <a href="http://www.religion-online.org/showarticle.asp?title=232">
      Reflections of an Ecclesiastical Expatriate
     </a>
     <br/>
     In:
     <i>
      The Christian Century
     </i>
     , October 24, 1990, pp. 964-9.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1997]
     <a href="http://www.religion-online.org/showarticle.asp?title=240">
      Epistemological Modesty: An Interview with Peter Berger
     </a>
     <br/>
     In:
     <i>
      The Christian Century
     </i>
     , October 29, 1997, pp. 972-8.
     <br/>
     Berger explains why the modern challenges is how to live with uncertainty. The basic fault lines today are not between people with different beliefs but between people who hold these beliefs with an element of uncertainty and people who hold these beliefs with a pretense of certitude. There is a middle ground between fanatism and relativism. Thats what is ment by epistemomogical modesty, you can belief certain things, but you are modest about these claims.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1998]
     <a href="http://www.religion-online.org/showarticle.asp?title=239">
      Protestantism and the Quest for Certainty
     </a>
     <br/>
     In:
     <i>
      The Christian Century
     </i>
     , August 26-September 2, 1998, pp. 782-796.
     <br/>
     Berger explains a big mistake he made in his career as a sociologist. He once believed that modernity necessarily leads to a decline in religion. But he also explains one big insight: pluralism undermines the taken-for-granted of beliefs and values. In the article the mistake and the insight are related.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     Kessel, David H.
     <br/>
     <a href="http://www.angelfire.com/or/sociologyshop/motifs.html">
      Bergers Motifs of Sociological Consiousness
     </a>
     <br/>
     Kessel explains his fascination for a classic in sociology, Bergers
     <i>
      Invitation to Sociology
     </i>
     . In a few word he stresses the importance of Bergers ideas about how to think sociologically...especially critically. The four motifs or themes of sociological consiousness are illustrated from Bergers book.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://www.angelfire.com/or/sociologyshop/PLB.html">
      Peter L. Berger Room
     </a>
     <br/>
     A listing of online publications on Peter Berger.
    </li>
   </ul>
   <br/>

      Raymond Boudon
     </a>
     (Acadmie des Sciences Morales et Politiques)
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1982]
     <a href="https://openlibrary.org/works/OL1704298W/Dictionnaire_critique_de_la_sociologie">
      Dictionnaire critique de la sociologie / A critical dictionary of sociology
     </a>
     - with Franois Bourricaud
     <p>

    </li>
    <li>
     [1990]
     <a href="http://skhole.fr/les-causes-de-l-in%C3%A9galit%C3%A9-des-chances-scolaires-par-raymond-boudon">
      Les causes de lingalit des chances scolaires
     </a>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1998]
     <a href="http://books.google.nl/books?id=FOAA_kbfbH8C&amp;pg=PA63#v=onepage">
      A satisfying theory of social knowledge
     </a>
     <br/>
     Why do people believe in fragile and false ideas? Boudon identifies three classical theories on this subject. He uses Herbert Simons satisficing theory of decision to explain the magical beliefs.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1999]
     <a href="http://books.google.nl/books/about/The_origin_of_values.html?id=Yvrzyht4RdcC">
      The origin of values
     </a>
     <br/>
     A critical discussion of relativistic theories of values. Boudon argues that rationalist theories of axiological feelings are scientifically much more valid and promising than culturalist or naturalist theories.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [2002]
     <a href="http://www.exergue.com/h/2012-07/tt/boudon-valeurs.html">
      Dclin de la morale, dclin des valeurs
     </a>
     <br/>
     Paris: PUF.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     Sperber, Dan [1997]
     <br/>
     <a href="http://www.dan.sperber.fr/?p=31">
      Individualisme mthodologique et cognitivisme
     </a>
     <br/>
     In: Boudon, Raymond / Chazel, Franois / Bouvier, Alban (eds.) [1997] Cognition et sciences sociales. Paris: Presse Universitaires de France.
     <p>
     </p>

     <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Raymond_Boudon">

     </a>

      Sociolgy is a Martial Art, part 1

     |
     <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=xFuAOP1H6Go&amp;feature=related">

     <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_V_X52VQcA8&amp;feature=related">
      6
     </a>
     |
     <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=0Ksp48lX8hw&amp;feature=related">
      7
     </a>
     (YouTube)
     <br/>
     A documentary about Bourdieus life. Filmed over three years, director Pierre Carles camera follows Bourdieu as he lectures, attends political rallies, travels, meets with his students, staff, and research team in Paris, and includes Bourdieu having a conversation with Gnter Grass.
The title of the film stresses the degree of Bourdieus political engagement. He took on the mantle of Emile Zola and Jean-Paul Sartre in French public life, slugging it out with politicians because he considered those lucky enough to have spent their lives studying the social world could not be indifferent to the struggle for justice. It is a vital documentary that is useful in various courses in social sciences.
     <p>

    <li>
     <a href="http://www.britannica.com/EBchecked/topic/125207/James-S-Coleman">

     </a>

     <a href="http://www.lutz-bornmann.de">
      Bornmann, Lutz
     </a>
     (Kiel, German)
     <br/>
     <a href="http://www.lutz-bornmann.de/luh/coleman.htm">
      Grundbegriffe und leitende Annahmen der Sozialtheorie James S. Coleman
     </a>
     <br/>
     A short article on the fundamental concepts and propositions of Colemans social theory.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://www.analyse-und-kritik.net/1992-2/inhalt.htm">

       Foundations of Social Theory
      </i>
      (92-2)

     ,

      (93-1)

      (93-2)

     <br/>

    <li>
     [1979]
     <a href="http://media.pfeiffer.edu/lridener/courses/COLLINR2.HTML">
      The Late Twentieth-Century Credential Crisis
     </a>
     <br/>

    </li>

     [1993]
     <a href="http://socrel.oxfordjournals.org/content/54/2/127.full.pdf">
      Liberals and Conservatives, Religious and Political: A Conjuncture of Modern History
     </a>
     <br/>
     In: Sociology of Religion 54(2):127.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1994]
     <a href="http://www.questia.com/read/52270669/four-sociological-traditions">
      Four Sociological Traditions
     </a>
     <br/>
     New York/Oxford: Oxford University Press.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1998]
     <a href="http://www.gsm.uci.edu/econsoc/Collins.html">
      The Multiple Fronts of Economic Sociology
     </a>
     --&gt; [MOVED TO ?}
     <br/>
     Editorial essay in the Economic Sociology Section of the ASA.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [2000] Waller, David V.:
     <a href="http://www.questia.com/read/14347187/questioning-geopolitics-political-projects-in-a-changing">
      Predictions of Geopolitical Theory and the Modern World-System
     </a>
     <br/>
     In: Derluguian, Georgi M. / Greer, Scott, L.: Questioning Geopolitics: Political Projects in a Changing World-System, Chapter 4.
     <br/>
     Westport/London: Praeger.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [2000]
     <a href="http://www.ssc.wisc.edu/theoryatmadison/papers/ivwCollins.pdf">
      Interview with Randall Collins
     </a>
     conducted by Alair Maclean &amp; James Yocom
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [2005]
     <a href="http://www.amazon.com/gp/product/0691123896/ref=as_li_tf_tl?ie=UTF8&amp;camp=1789&amp;creative=9325&amp;creativeASIN=0691123896&amp;linkCode=as2&amp;tag=sociosite-20">
      Interaction Ritual Chains
     </a>
     <img alt="" border="0" height="1" src="http://ir-na.amazon-adsystem.com/e/ir?t=sociosite-20&amp;l=as2&amp;o=1&amp;a=0691123896" style="border:none !important; margin:0px !important;" width="1"/>
     [Amazon]
     <br/>
     Princeton University Press
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      Collins presents a theory that attempts to develop a radical microsociology. Successful rituals create symbols of group membership and pump up individuals with emotional energy, while failed rituals drain emotional energy. Each person flows from situation to situation, drawn to those interactions where their cultural capital gives them the best emotional energy payoff. Thinking, too, can be explained by the internalization of conversations within the flow of situations; individual selves are thoroughly and continually social, constructed from the outside in.
      <br/>
      The first half of this book is based on the classic analyses of Durkheim, Mead, and Goffman and draws on micro-sociological research on conversation, bodily rhythms, emotions, and intellectual creativity. The second half discusses how such activities as sex, smoking, and social stratification are shaped by interaction ritual chains. Collins addresses the emotional and symbolic nature of sexual exchanges of all sorts: from hand-holding to masturbation to sexual relationships with prostitutes.
     </span>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [2005]
     <a href="http://www.cjsonline.ca/reviews/interactionritual.html">
      The Sociology of Almost Everything
     </a>
     <br/>
     Four Questions to Randall Collins about Interaction Ritual Chains.
     <br/>
     In: Canadian Journal of Sociology Online, January-February 2005.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [2009]
     <a href="http://www.amazon.com/gp/product/0691143226/ref=as_li_tf_tl?ie=UTF8&amp;camp=1789&amp;creative=9325&amp;creativeASIN=0691143226&amp;linkCode=as2&amp;tag=sociosite-20">
      Violence: A Micro-sociological Theory
     </a>
     <img alt="" border="0" height="1" src="http://ir-na.amazon-adsystem.com/e/ir?t=sociosite-20&amp;l=as2&amp;o=1&amp;a=0691143226" style="border:none !important; margin:0px !important;" width="1"/>
     [Amazon]
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      Collins gives a comprehensive explanation of violence and its dynamics, drawing upon video footage, cutting-edge forensics, and ethnography to examine violent situations up close as they actually happen. Violence comes neither easily nor automatically. Antagonists are by nature tense and fearful, and their confrontational anxieties put up a powerful emotional barrier against violence. We are guided into the disturbing worlds of human discord: domestic abuse, schoolyard bullying, muggings, violent sports, and armed conflicts. The
      <i>
       fog of war
      </i>
      pervades all violent encounters, limiting people mostly to bluster and bluff, and making violence, when it does occur, largely incompetent, often injuring someone other than its intended target. Violence can be triggered only when pathways around this emotional barrier are presented. Collins explains why violence typically comes in the form of atrocities against the weak, ritualized exhibitions before audiences, or clandestine acts of terrorism and murder  and why a small number of individuals are competent at violence.
     </span>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [2011]
     <a href="http://sociological-eye.blogspot.com/2011/01/interaction-rituals-and-new-electronic.html">
      Interaction Rituals and the New Electronic Media
     </a>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://de.wikibooks.org/wiki/Soziologische_Klassiker/_Collins,_Randall">
      Soziologische Klassiker / Collins, Randall
     </a>
     (in wikibooks)
    </li>
   </ul>

       <b>
        Dahrendorf, Ralf
       </b>
       [1929 - 2009]
      </font>
     </td>
    </tr>
   </table>
   <p>
    <b>
     By Dahrendorf
    </b>
   </p>
   <ul>
    <img align="right" alt="Ralph Dahrendorf" height="232" src="../images/sociologists/dahrendorf.jpg" width="182"/>
    <li>
     [1959]
     <a href="http://archive.org/stream/classclassconfli00dahr/classclassconfli00dahr_djvu.txt">

     Stanford CA: Stanford University Press.
     <p>

    </li>
    <li>
     [1959]
     <a href="http://media.pfeiffer.edu/lridener/courses/DAHREND1.HTML">
      Classes in Post-Capitalist Society
     </a>
     [extract]
     <br/>
     From: Ralf Dahrendorf [1959]
     <i>
      Class and Class Conflict in Industrial Society.
     </i>
     Stanford, CA: Stanford University Press, pp. 241-8.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1968]
     <a href="texts/dahrendorf.pdf">
      Gibt es noch Klassen?
     </a>
     - Die Begriffe der sozialen Schicht und sozialen Klasse in der Sozialanalyse der Gegenwart [pdf] or
     <a href="texts/dahrendorf.php">
      html
     </a>
     <br/>
     In: Seidel, B. / Jenkner, S. [1968] Klassenbildung und Sozialschichtung. Darmstadt, pp. 279-96.
    </li>
   </ul>
   <p>
    <b>
     Dahrendorf Live
    </b>
   </p>
   <ul>
    <li>
     [1989]
     <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Yj5pVe6hOZo">
      Reflections from a Life in Science and Politics
     </a>
     [50:31]
     <br/>
     Dahrendorf talks with UC Berkeleys Harry Kreisler about his formative experiences and the ideas that have shaped in career in the academy and in public service. Recorded April 4, 1989.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [2008]
     <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=lnoTyC2gI5g">
      50 Jahre fr die Freiheit
     </a>
     <br/>
     Die Friedrich-Naumann-Stiftung fr die Freiheit feierte am 19. Mai 2008 ihren 50. Geburtstag mit einem Festakt in Bonn.
    </li>
   </ul>
   <p>
    <b>
     On Dahrendorf
    </b>
   </p>
   <ul>
    <li>
     Schnellenbach, Jan [2005]
     <br/>
     <a href="http://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=904711">
      The Dahrendorf hypothesis and its implications for (the theory of) economic policy-making
     </a>
     <br/>
     In: Cambridge Journal of Economics, 29(6): 997-1009.
     <br/>
     According to Dahrendorf, a principal commitment to the market order and to an open society protecting individual liberties is largely undisputed today, at least in the western hemisphere. This, however, does not result in a convergence of economic policies towards some common ideal model. This hypothesis is difficult to reconcile with orthodox economic approaches to economic policy-making. Schellenbach argues that if the scope of analysis is extended to epistemological problems, then a decentralised experimentation with policies can be reasonable. Given the fact that selection mechanisms are imperfect and that diversity can be sustained, a dilemma may occur for neoclassical economics: if individuals internalise preferences for inefficient policy measures, a decision between the sovereignty of individual preferences and efficiency may have to be made.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ralf_Dahrendorf">
      Wikipedia: Ralf_Dahrendorf
     </a>
    </li>
   </ul>

   <b>
    Biography &amp; Bibliography
   </b>
   <ul>
    <li>
     <a href="http://www.thomashampson.com/1997/02/02/william-edward-burghardt-du-bois-1868-1963/">
      Biography
     </a>
     - Thomas Hampson
     <p>

    <li>
     <a href="http://www.duboislc.org/book/export/html/99">
      A Biographical SKetch
     </a>
     - Gerald C. Hynes
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://www.library.umass.edu/spcoll/collections/dubois/">
      Du Bois central
     </a>
     <br/>
     A site is maintained by the Department of Special Collections and University Archives at University of Massachusetts Amherst to commemorate the life and legacy of the pioneering sociologist, playwright, and black civil rights campaigner W. E. B. Du Bois. It offers free access to a biography, photographs and large collection of digital materials relating to Du Bois. A particular strength is the online collection of ebooks and articles by Du Bois. These include key writings on the evolution of Negro leadership, racism in America in the 19th and early 20th centuries and the formation of black civil rights movements in the USA .
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://www.webdubois.org">
      WebDuBois.org
     </a>
     <br/>
     Annotated links to online writings by and about W.E.B. Du Bois. The site includes links to works of social science, literature, biography, and political activism. Editor: Robert W. Williams.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://www.cviog.uga.edu/Projects/gainfo/dubois2.htm">

    <li>

   <p>
    <b>
     General Sources
    </b>
   </p>
   <ul>
    <img align="right" alt="Emile Durkheim" src="../images/sociologists/durkheim.gif"/>
    <li>
     <a href="http://durkheim.uchicago.edu/">
      The Durkheim Pages
     </a>
     <br/>
     Devoted to the presentation of information concerning Emile Durkheim. Contents: Some full texts, a complete bibliography of Durkheims work, a timeline describing important events related to Durkheim and the Third French Republic, a glossary of terms and concepts, a bibliography of secondary material, news, a list of Durkheim scholars and mailing list, and
     <i>
      Durkheimian Studies
     </i>
     information. Editor: Robert Alun Jones (Univ. of Illinois, USA).
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://durkheim.itgo.com">
      Emile Durkheim
     </a>
     <br/>
     An informative undergraduate source for the thought, concepts, and writings of Durkheim. Contains both secondary information and over 70 Durkheim quotes on various topics linke anomie, religion, the division of labor, suicide, and others. Editor: L. Joe Dunman (Murray State University, USA).
    </li>
   </ul>

      English abstract of the recently discovered transcript of Durkheims philosophy lectures at the Lye de Sens. Student lecture notes taken from Durkheims course on philosophy in 1883-84. Notes taken by the French philosopher, Andre Lalande. Written manuscript: Bibliothque de la Sorbonne, Manuscript 2351. [
      <a href="http://durkheim.uchicago.edu/Texts/1884a/tr.abst.html">
       abstract
      </a>

    </li>
    <li>
     [1912] The Elementary Forms of the Religious Life
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="https://www.questia.com/read/587580/the-elementary-forms-of-the-religious-life">
        Complete Version
       </a>
       <br/>
       Published by The Free Press, New York. Translated from the French by Joseph Ward Swain.
       <p>
       </p>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="http://durkheim.uchicago.edu/Summaries/forms.html">
        Excerpt
       </a>
       from Robert Alun Jones,
       <i>
        Emile Durkheim: An Introduction to Four Major Works.
       </i>
       Beverly Hills. CA: Sage Publications, Inc. 1986, pp. 115-155.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1914]
     <a href="http://www.marxists.org/reference/subject/philosophy/works/fr/durkheim.htm">
      Pragmatism &amp; the Question of Truth
     </a>
     <br/>
     Pragmatism is much less of an undertaking to encourage action than an attack on pure speculation and theoretical thought. Durkheim discusses the pragmatist doctrines (such as everything is reducable to a purely logical construction) and establishes a parallel between pragmatism and sociology.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1921]
     <a href="texts/durkheim.pdf">

     </a>
     [pdf]

    </li>
    <li>
     [1950] Leons de sociologie: physique des moeurs et du droit.
     <br/>

     <br/>

     <a href="https://openlibrary.org/works/OL606927W/Lec%CC%A7ons_de_sociologie">

     </a>

     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>

   <ul>
    <li>
     [1967/84]
     <a href="http://www.foucault.info/documents/heterotopia/foucault.heterotopia.en.html">
      Of other Spaces: Heterotopias
     </a>
     [English and French version]
     <br/>
     From: Dits et crits 1984: 46-9. Translated from the French by Jay Miskowiec.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1969]
     <a href="http://www.foucault.info/documents/archaeologyofknowledge/foucault.archaeologyofknowledge.00_intro.html">
      The Archaeology of Knowledge
     </a>
     <br/>
     Introduction and first three chapters:
     <i>
      The Unities of Discourse
     </i>
     ;
     <i>
      Discursive Formations
     </i>
     and
     <i>
      The Formation of Objects
     </i>
     .
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1973]
     <a href="http://www.amazon.com/gp/product/0803268572/ref=as_li_tf_tl?ie=UTF8&amp;camp=1789&amp;creative=9325&amp;creativeASIN=0803268572&amp;linkCode=as2&amp;tag=sociosite-20">
      I, Pierre Riviere, having slaughtered my mother, my sister, and my brother: A Case of Parricide in the 19th Century
     </a>
     <img alt="" border="0" height="1" src="http://ir-na.amazon-adsystem.com/e/ir?t=sociosite-20&amp;l=as2&amp;o=1&amp;a=0803268572" style="border:none !important; margin:0px !important;" width="1"/>
     [Amazon] -
     <a href="http://books.google.nl/books?id=5uRVWs6kbiYC">
      Google Books
     </a>
     <br/>
     See also the
     <a href="http://www.foucault.info/documents/pierreRiviere/foucault.pierreRiviere.interview.en.html">
      interview
     </a>
     (1976) with Foucault on this book.
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      To free his father and himself from his mother's tyranny, Pierre Rivire decided to kill her. On June 3,1835, he went inside his small Normandy house with a pruning hook and cut to death his mother, his eighteen-year-old sister, and his seven-year-old brother. Then, in jail, he wrote a memoir to justify the whole gruesome tale. Michel Foucault collected the relevant documents of the case, including medical and legal testimony, police records, and Rivires memoir. The Rivire case occurred at a time when many professions were contending for status and power. Medical authority was challenging law, branches of government were vying. Foucaults reconstruction of the case is a brilliant exploration of the roots of our contemporary views of madness, justice, and crime.
     </span>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1974]
     <a href="http://www.foucault.info/documents/foucault.truthAndJudicialForms.en.html">
      Truth and Judicial Forms
     </a>
     [Excerpt}
     <br/>
     In: Power: Essential Works of Foucault, 1954-1984: Volume Three. Translated by Robert Hurley. The New York Press.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1974]
     <a href="http://www.foucault-studies.com/no1/foucault1.pdf">
      The Crisis of Medicine or the Crisis of Antimedicine?
     </a>
     <br/>
     <i>
      Foucault Studies
     </i>
     , 1:5-19, December 2004.
     <br/>
     Translated by Edgar C. Knowlton, Jr., William J. King, and Clare OFarrell.
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      The first lecture given by Foucault on social medicine in Oktober 1974 at the Institute of Social Medicine, Biomedical Center, of the State University of Rio de Janeiro, Brazil. Originally published in Portuguese translation as Crisis de un modelo en la medicina?,
      <i>
       Revista centroamericana de Ciencas de la Salud
      </i>
      , 3, January-April 1976, pp. 197-209; and in Spanish as La crisis de la medicina o la crisis de la antimedicina,
      <i>
       Educacion Medica y Salud
      </i>
      , 10(2), 1976, pp. 152-70. The version in
      <i>
       Dits et crits
      </i>
      , (Paris, Gallimard), vol. III, pp. 40-58 is a retranslation of the Portuguese back into French.
     </span>

    </li>
    <li>
     Olivier, Lawrence / Nol, Roger [1993]
     <br/>
     <a href="http://1libertaire.free.fr/Foucault16.html">
      Michel Foucault: problmatique pour une histoire de lhomosexualit
     </a>
     <br/>
     In: Revue sexuologique / Sexological Review 2(1).
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      The history of homosexuality has primarily focused on two aspects: the process of repression/liberation and the problematic of the historical development of homosexuality. By emphasizing the oppression experienced by the gay community and by studying homosexuality as part of a larger history of sexuality, both approaches have neglected significant elements of the specific history of homosexuality.  Foucaults work on homosexuality enables us to rethink the way this history has been presented by demonstrating that repression has not been the mechanism through which perverse sexuality has been controlled in western societies. Foucault also suggests that the concept of friendship emerging in the West during the 17th and 18th centuries may prove to be an auspicious avenue to explore when seeking a fuller history of homosexuality. Lastly, Foucault opens new perspectives on political practices. In contrast with the politics of liberation, he suggests putting liberty at the center of ones sexuality (homosexuality), an alternative that seeks to create new social relationships, a new culture.
     </span>
     <p>
     </p>

      German
     </a>
     version)
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1959]
     <a href="http://opus4.kobv.de/opus4-Fromm/files/1000/1959f-eng.pdf">
      Freedom in the Work Situation
     </a>
     <br/>
     In: Michael Harrington and Paul Jacobs (eds.) [1959]
     <i>
      Labor in a Free Society
     </i>
     , pp. 1-16.
     <br/>
     Fromm explains why the only alternative to the danger of robotism is humanistic communitarianism. Changes in ownership must be made to the extent necessary to create a community of work, and to prevent the profit motive from directing production into socially harmful directions. Income must be equalized to the extent of giving everybody the material basis for a dignified life and thus preventing economic differences from creating a fundamentally different experience of life among various social classes. Man must be reinstituted in his supreme place in society - never a means, never a thing to be used by others or by himself.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1962]
     <a href="http://www.transhumanism.org/index.php/WTA/more/frommcredo">
      Erich Fromms Humanist Credo
     </a>
     <br/>
     In: Erich Fromm [1962]
     <i>
      Beyond the Chains of Illusions
     </i>
     New York: Simon and Schuster, pp. 174-82.
     <br/>
     Fromm explains what he believes in. I believe in freedom, in mens right to be himself, to assert himself and to fight all those who try to prevent him from being himself. But freedom is more thant the absense of violent oppression. It is more than freedom from. It is freedom to - the freedom to become independent; the freedom to be much, rather than to have much, or to use things and people.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1974]
     <a href="http://opus4.kobv.de/opus4-Fromm/frontdoor/index/index/docId/13089">
      Die Zukunft des Menschen und die Frage der Destruktivitt
     </a>
     [pdf]
     <br/>
     Interview by Robert Jungk on Fromms contribution to the question of human agression.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://www.fromm-gesellschaft.de/">
      Internationale Erich Fromm Gesellschaft
     </a>
     <br/>
     A site from Germany that includes a biography, a bibliography, online reading, and a Fromm forum. Some of the articles are original writings by Fromm some of which were published here for the first time. The collection of secondary literature about Fromm comprises papers previously published in the Yearbooks of the Erich Fromm Society or that were printed in the Fromm Forum. The best place to start. In German and English language.
    </li>
   </ul>

    <b>
     <i>
      On Fromm
     </i>
    </b>
   </p>
   <ul>
    <li>
     McLaughlin, Neil [1999] (Department of Sociology, McMaster University, Canada)
     <a href="http://www.ualberta.ca/~cjscopy/articles/mclaughlin.html">
      Origin Myths in the Social Sciences: Fromm, the Frankfurt School and the Emergence of Critical Theory
     </a>
     <br/>
     <i>
      Canadian Journal of Sociology
     </i>
     24(1): 109-39.

   <p>
    <a name="GARFINKEL">
    </a>
   </p>
   <table cellpadding="4" width="100%">
    <tr>
     <td bgcolor="#336699">
      <a href="#top" target="_self">
       <img align="right" alt="Index" border="0" src="../images/up.gif"/>
      </a>
      <font color="WHITE" size="2">
       <b>
        Garfinkel, Harold
       </b>
       [1917 - 2011]
      </font>
     </td>
    </tr>
   </table>
   <ul>
    <p>
     <img align="right" alt="Harold Garfinkel" src="../images/sociologists/garfinkel.jpg"/>
    </p>
    <li>
     <a href="http://www.soc.ucla.edu/faculty.php?lid=1308&amp;display_one=1">
      UCLA page of Garfinkel
     </a>
     - University of California, Los Angeles
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1967]
     <a href="http://media.pfeiffer.edu/lridener/courses/ethnomet.html">
      Some essential features of common understandings
     </a>
     (summary)
     <br/>
     From: Harold Garfinkel [1967/84]
     <i>
      Studies in Ethnomethodology.
     </i>
     Englewood Cliffs, NJ: Prentice-Hall,  pp. 38-44, 75.
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      Garfinkel demonstrates why common understandings cannot possibly consist of a measured amount of shared agreement among persons on certain topics. In many matters we can understand each other in a conversation although we dont mention the specific matters. We can understand certain matters on the basis of not only of what was actually said but what was left unspoken. Garfinkel explains how this is possible and how professional sociologists can rediscover common sense knowledge and common sense activities.
     </span>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1967]
     <a href="texts/goffman_self.php">
      Studies of the Routine Grounds of Everyday Activities
     </a>
     (summary)
     <br/>
     From: Harold Garfinkel [1967]
     <i>
      Studies in Ethnomethodology.
     </i>
     Polity Press, ch. 2.
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      When you examine the exchange that takes place in a conversation between two people, you realize soon that there are many things that are understood between these people than are actually mentioned (especially when these people have a standing relationship). We can understand matters on the basis of what was unspoken. This points to underlying properties of conversational exchange and the rules which govern them in daily live. The rule governed activities of everyday life constitute the moral order of society. To test his hypotheses, Garfinkel sent forth a legion of students to conduct conversational breaching experiments. Students were instructed to question everything they were being told by asking what was meant. One of the conclusions of these experiments was that background knowledge is important and is understood as such when it is shared. However, the possibility of common understanding does not consist in demonstrated measures of shared knowledge of social structure, but consists instead and entirely in the enforceable character of actions in compliance with the exigencies of everyday life as a morality. Common sense knowledge of the facts of social life for the members of the society is institutionalized knowledge of the real world.
     </span>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [2007]
     <a href="http://books.google.nl/books?id=ZimNMN794A0C&amp;printsec=frontcover&amp;source=gbs_v2_summary_r&amp;cad=0">
      Four releations between literatures of the social scientific movement and their specific ethnomethodological alternates
     </a>
     <br/>
     In: Stephen Hester / David Francis (eds.) [2007] Orders of Ordinary Action - Respecifying Sociological Knowledge.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="theory.php#ETHNOMETH">
      Ethnomethodology
      <sub>
       <img border="0" src="../images/next.gif"/>
      </sub>
     </a>
     (SocioSite)
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     Obituary in
     <a href="http://newsroom.ucla.edu/faculty-bulletin-board/in-memoriam:-sociologist-harold-garfinkel">
      UCLA
     </a>
     [28.4.2011] |
     <a href="http://www.asanet.org/footnotes/septoct11/obit_0911.html#obit_1">
      ASA
     </a>
     |
     <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2011/05/04/us/04garfinkel.html">
      New York Times
     </a>
     [3.5.2011] |
     <a href="http://www.theguardian.com/education/2011/jul/13/harold-garfinkel-obituary">
      Guardian
     </a>
     [13.6.2011]
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harold_Garfinkel">
      Wikipedia: Harold_Garfinkel
     </a>
    </li>
   </ul>
   <p>
    <a name="GEERTZ">
    </a>
   </p>
   <table cellpadding="4" width="100%">
    <tr>
     <td bgcolor="#336699">
      <a href="#top" target="_self">
       <img align="right" alt="Index" border="0" src="../images/up.gif"/>
      </a>
      <font color="WHITE" size="2">
       <b>
        Geertz, Clifford
       </b>
       [1926 - 2006]
      </font>
     </td>
    </tr>
   </table>
   <ul>
    <img align="right" alt="Clifford Geertz" height="293" src="../images/sociologists/geertz.gif" width="171"/>
    <li>
     YouTube:
     <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=3dQDx3axrDs">
      Interview with Clifford Geertz, part 1
     </a>
     |
     <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=fcJ8kOQU7Mg">
      part 2
     </a>
     <br/>
     Filmed in May 2004 in Cambridge.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://hypergeertz.jku.at/">
      HyperGeertz - WorldCatalogue
     </a>
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      A comprehensive, contextual and referential bibliography and mediagraphy of the works and public statements by one of the most important social scientists of our time, Clifford Geertz. Presented by Ingo Mrth and Gerhard Frhlich (Johannes Kepler Universitt Linz, Austria).
     </span>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1964]
     <a href="texts/geertz_ideology.php">
      Ideology as a Cultural System
     </a>
     <br/>
     In: Apter, David Ernest (ed.) [1964] Ideology and discontent. New York: Glencoe. pp. 47-76.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1973]
     <a href="http://condor.depaul.edu/dweinste/popcult/geertz.html">
      Emphasizing Interpretation
     </a>
     <br/>
     From: C. Geertz [1973]
     <i>
      The Interpretation of Cultures.
     </i>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1973]
     <a href="texts/Geertz_Thick_Description.php">
      Thick Description: Toward an Interpretive Theory of Culture
     </a>
     <br/>
     In: Geertz [1973] The Interpretation of cultures: selected essays. New York: Basis Books. pp. 3-30.
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      Man is an animal suspended in webs of significance he himself has spun. Geertzs concept of culture is -in the good old tradition of Max Weber- is essentially a semiotic one. Culture is a complex web of significance, and therefor its analysis is no a matter of experimental science in search of law, but an interpretive science in search of meaning. Geertz borrows the notion of thick description from Gilbert Ryle to grasp the meaning(s) of culture(s).
     </span>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1976]
     <a href="http://hypergeertz.jku.at/GeertzTexts/Art_Cultural.htm">
      Art as a Cultural System
     </a>
     <br/>
     In: Modern Language Notes 91(6): 1473-99.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1991]
     <a href="http://www.jacweb.org/Archived_volumes/Text_articles/V11_I2_OlsonGeertz.htm">
      Clifford Geertz on ethnography and social construction
     </a>
     <br/>
     Interview by Gary A. Olson, in Journal of Advanced Composition. 11(2): 245-68.
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      Why is Geertzs work so eminently appealing to many of us? It might be because his preoccupation with seeing science and scholarship as
      <i>
       rethorical
      </i>
      , as socially constructed.
     </span>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1999]
     <a href="http://www.acls.org/publications/op/haskins/1999_cliffordgeertz.pdf">
      A Life of Learning
     </a>
     <br/>
     Charles Homer Haskins Lectur for 1999. American Council of Learned Societies, Occasional Paper No. 45.
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      In the course of patching together his scholarly career Geertz has learned at least one thing: it all depends on the timing. In his improvised life he discovered that the study of cultures involves discovering who they think they are, what they think they are doing, and to what end they think that they are doing it. Therefore it is necessary to gain a working familiarity with the frames of meaning whitin which they enacht their lives.
     </span>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [2000]
     <a href="http://www.pup.princeton.edu/chapters/s6780.html">
      Passage and Accident: A Life of Learning
     </a>
     <br/>
     Chapter I of Available Light: Anthropological Reflections on Philosophical Topics
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [2000, May]
     <a href="http://www.nybooks.com/articles/archives/2000/may/11/indonesia-starting-over/?pagination=false">
      Indonesia: Starting Over
     </a>
     <br/>
     In: The New York Review of Books, May 11, 2000.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [2000]
     <a href="http://hypergeertz.jku.at/GeertzTexts/Interview_Micheelsen.htm">
      I dont do systems
     </a>
     <br/>
     Interview with Geertz by Arun Micheelsen.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [2001]
     <a href="http://press.princeton.edu/titles/9145.html">
      Life Among the Anthros
     </a>
     <br/>
     A review of Darkness in Eldorado: How Scientists and Journalists Devastated the Amazon, by Patrick Tierney. In: The New York Review of Books, February 8, 2001.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [2004]
     <a href="http://www.alanmacfarlane.com/ancestors/geertz.htm">
      Frazer Lecture
     </a>
     [video]
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [2006]
     <a href="http://books.google.nl/books?id=BZ1BmKEHti0C&amp;dq=clifford+geertz&amp;source=bl&amp;ots=w8FG86-Dv5&amp;sig=VrDcr2Pu7u-sSiC_2-Aac-HF6fc&amp;hl=nl&amp;ei=wVhXTNuTHNOOOLWJrZ4O&amp;sa=X&amp;oi=book_result&amp;ct=result&amp;resnum=11&amp;ved=0CEMQ6AEwCg">
      The interpretation of cultures: selected essays
     </a>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     Windschuttle, Keith [2002]
     <br/>
     <a href="http://www.newcriterion.com/articles.cfm/The-ethnocentrism-of-Clifford-Geertz--1889">
      The ethnocentrism of Clifford Geertz
     </a>
     <br/>
     In: The New Criterion, 21(2)
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      Article on Geertz
      <i>
       Available Light: Anthropological Reflections on Philosophical Topics
      </i>
      . He discusses Geertz concept of
      <i>
       thick description
      </i>
      : human gestures often have multiple layers of meaning that can only be described through the symbols used by a culture. Together with sharp descriptions of all his other contributions, it is an intellectual portrait of Geertz.
     </span>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     Yarrow, Andres L. [2006]
     <br/>
     <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2006/11/01/obituaries/01geertz.html">
      Clifford Geertz, Cultural Anthropologist, Is Dead at 80
     </a>
     <br/>
     New York Times, november 1, 2006.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clifford_Geertz">
      Wikipedia: Clifford Geertz
     </a>
    </li>
   </ul>

      The Second Globalization Debate
     </a>
     <br/>
     A talk with Anthony Giddens by John Brockman.
     <br/>
     In: Edge - The Third Culture.
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      The driving force of the new globalization is the communications revolution, Beyond its effects on the individual, this revolution is fundamentally altering the way public institutions interact. Risk is an essential component of this future-oriented environment. Scientific innovation explores the edge between the positive and negative sides of risk. Risk management, then, becomes a necessary a field of analysis.
     </span>

    <a name="GOFFMAN">
    </a>
   </p>
   <table cellpadding="4" width="100%">
    <tr>
     <td bgcolor="#336699">
      <a href="#top" target="_self">
       <img align="right" alt="Index" border="0" src="../images/up.gif"/>
      </a>
      <font color="WHITE" size="2">
       <b>
        Goffman, Erving
       </b>
       [1922 - 1982]
      </font>
     </td>
    </tr>
   </table>
   <ul>
    <img align="right" alt="Erving Goffman" src="../images/sociologists/goffman_erving.bmp"/>
    <li>
     <a href="http://people.brandeis.edu/~teuber/goffmanbio.html">
      Erving Goffman Biography
     </a>
     <br/>
     Part of The Goffman Reader, by: Charles Lemert and Ann Branaman.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://www.tau.ac.il/~algazi/mat/goffman.htm">
      Goffman Biography
     </a>
     <br/>
     by Gadi Algazi (Tel Aviv University, Israel)
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://www.blackwood.org/Erving.htm">
      Erving Goffmann
     </a>
     by B. Diane Blackwood
     <br/>
     Article for
     <i>
      Magills Guide to the 20th Century Authors
     </i>
     (1977) Pasadena, CA: Salem Press.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1959] The Presentation of Self in Everyday Life |
     <a href="http://www.amazon.com/gp/product/0140135715/ref=as_li_tf_tl?ie=UTF8&amp;camp=1789&amp;creative=9325&amp;creativeASIN=0140135715&amp;linkCode=as2&amp;tag=sociosite-20">
      Amazon
     </a>
     <img alt="" border="0" height="1" src="http://ir-na.amazon-adsystem.com/e/ir?t=sociosite-20&amp;l=as2&amp;o=1&amp;a=0140135715" style="border:none !important; margin:0px !important;" width="1"/>
     <br/>
     New York: Doubleday
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      Goffman analyses the structures of social encounters from the perspective of the dramatic performance. He shows how people use such
      <i>
       fixed props
      </i>
      as houses, clothes, and job situations; how they combine in teams resembling secret societies; and, how they adopt discrepant roles and communicate out of character. Goffman takes us also
      <i>
       backstage
      </i>
      , into the regions where people both prepare their images and relax from them; and he demonstrates in painful detail what can happen when a performance falls flat.
     </span>
     <ul style="margin-left:1.6em; padding-left:0.0em" type="square">
      <table align="right" bgcolor="#336699" border="0" cellpadding="4" cellspacing="1" width="252">
       <tr bgcolor="#ffffff">
        <td>
         <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
          Society is organized on the principle that any individual who possesses certain social characteristics has a moral right to expect that others will value and treat him in an appropriate way. Connected with this principle is a second, namely that an individual who implicitly or explicitly signifies he has certain social characteristics ought in fact to be what he claims he is [p. 11].
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>

      </p>

      <li>
       [1995]
       <a href="http://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/BF02390165#page-1">

      <li>

     <a href="http://link.springer.com/article/10.1007%2FBF02390165#page-1">
      The Dramaturgical Society: Macro-Analytic Approach to  Dramaturgical Analysis
     </a>
     <br/>
     The writings of Goffman have illuminated the dramaturgical components of face-to-face interaction. But the task of developing a  substantive and theoretical explication of the dramaturgical society remains. Young &amp; Massey try to expand the dramaturgical analysis to the macro-societal.

   <ul>
    <img align="right" alt="Johan Goudsblom" src="../images/sociologists/goudsblom.gif"/>
    <li>
     <a href="http://nl.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johan_Goudsblom">
      Wikipedia: Johan Goudsblom
     </a>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="goudsblom.php">
      Bibliography
     </a>
     - by Willem Kranendonk
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1969]
     <a href="goudsblom_elias.php">
      Interview with Norbert Elias
     </a>
     <!--[&lt;a href="http://www.kuwi.uni-linz.ac.at/hyperelias/z-elias/abstracts/FullText-eng-1970-I-eng-1.htm"&gt;mirror&lt;/a&gt;]-->
     <br/>
     In: Sociologische Gids 17(2): 133-40.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1989]
     <a href="goudsblom_20years.php">
      Twenty Years of Figurational Sociology in the Netherlands [1969-1989]
     </a>
     <br/>
     In:
     <i>
      Society as Process. A Bibliography of Figurational Sociology in the Netherlands (up to 1989)
     </i>
     . Amsterdam 1990.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1992]
     <a href="http://www.amazon.com/gp/product/0140157972/ref=as_li_tf_tl?ie=UTF8&amp;camp=1789&amp;creative=9325&amp;creativeASIN=0140157972&amp;linkCode=as2&amp;tag=sociosite-20">
      Fire and Civilization
     </a>
     <img alt="" border="0" height="1" src="http://ir-na.amazon-adsystem.com/e/ir?t=sociosite-20&amp;l=as2&amp;o=1&amp;a=0140157972" style="border:none !important; margin:0px !important;" width="1"/>
     [Amazon]
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      People became truly human by learning to domesticate fire and cook food. Other great transformation  the birth of agriculture and the industrial revolution  represent huge leaps forward in our relations with fire. Goudsblom examines Homer and the Hebrew Bible, the Vestal Virgins and pioneering Roman fire brigades, the role of Hell-fire in Christianitys civilizing campaign and developments from the age of steam to fire-powered cars and nuclear fusion. Even today, bush and forest fires caused by short-sighted farming policies or sheer high spirits contribute to the disastrous international wood shortage. This work of wide-ranging scholarship both illuminates such current concerns, and makes readers look again at the whole course of human history.
     </span>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=N9cMkWnaEZ0">
      Botsende beschavingen
     </a>
     [30:01]
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      Anil Ramdas talks with Johan Goudsblom, Arend-Jan and Pieter Boekestijn and Pieter Pekelharing about clash of civilizations based on Samuel Huntingtons article
      <i>
       Clash of Civilisations
      </i>
      . In the political debate is always about mass immigration from Muslim countries and the so-called Islamization of European culture. Where does this fear come from and does Western culture really needs protection?
     </span>
    </li>
   </ul>

    <li>
     Thornton, Alinta (University of Technology, Sydney, Australia)
     <br/>
     <a href="http://www.zipworld.com.au/~athornto/">
      Does Internet Create Democracy?
     </a>
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      A thesis on whether internet creates or contributes to democracy. She relates the internet and democracy debate to American communications theory (starting with Howard Rheingolds prediction that internet will facilitate an explosion of direct participatory democracy), and also explores some European theory, including Habermas. Thornton concludes that internet provides opportunities for limited revitalisation of the public sphere. These new opportunities are limited to privileged groups, but it is at least an increase in the activities of the public sphere, however modest.
     </span>
    </li>

      Pour une sociologie de la mmoire collective
     </a>
     <p>

    <li>
     Marce, Jean-Cristophe / Mucchielli, Laurant [1999]
     <br/>

     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://www.revuedumauss.com">
      Revue du Mauss
     </a>
     <br/>
     An anti-utilitarist journal that publishes social scientific contributions from different perspectives. The tribute to Marcesl Mauss indicates that theres a certain accent on the idea that social gift relations unite human subjects.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     Sabourin, Paul [1997]
     <br/>
     <a href="http://www.erudit.org/revue/socsoc/1997/v29/n2/001661ar.pdf">
      Perspective sur la mmoire sociale de Maurice Halbwachs
     </a>
     [pdf]
     <br/>
     In: Sociologie et socits, 29(2): 139-61.
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      A re-reading of Halbwachs work that examines the study of social memory as a contribution of note to social morphology, both from the point of view of defining problems and that of proposed solutions. His sociology of memory is a sociology of social experience. The author suggests that this sociology constitutes a theory of socialization that reveals the means of social appropriation of human experience through organizing and defining relationships among collective memories.
     </span>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     Topalov, Christian e.o [1999]

     </a>
     <br/>
     Revue dhistoire des sciences humaines, 1.

      Hall focuses on the concept of representation one of the key ideas of cultural studies and shows how reality is never experienced directly, but always through the symbolic categories made available by society.

    <li>
     [2006]
     <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=bMo2uiRAf30&amp;feature=related">
      Race, the Floating Signifier, part 1
     </a>
     [5:06],

      2
     </a>
     [9:29],
     <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=O-VyReHOVSc&amp;feature=related">
      3
     </a>
     [7:48],
     <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=lXQ-W7Up0p8&amp;feature=related">
      4
     </a>
     [10:18],
     <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=OVjmbDbnJKo">
      5
     </a>
     [9:59],
     <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=GeD6awgSHGU">
      6
     </a>
     [9:59],
     <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=vRRQ2KSBeyA">
      7
     </a>
     [2:52].
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      Hall argues against the biological interpretation of racial difference, He asks us to pay close attention to the cultural processes by which the visible differences of appearance come to stand for natural or biological properties of human beings. Drawing upon the work of writers such as Frantz Fanon, he shows how race is a discursive construct and, because its meaning is never fixed, can be described as a floating signifier.
     </span>

   <table cellpadding="4" width="100%">
    <tr>
     <td bgcolor="#336699">
      <a href="#top" target="_self">
       <img align="right" alt="Index" border="0" src="../images/up.gif"/>
      </a>
      <font color="WHITE" size="2">
       <b>
        Haraway, Donna J.
       </b>
       [1944]
      </font>
     </td>
    </tr>
   </table>
   <p>
    <b>
     By Haraway
    </b>
   </p>
   <ul>
    <img align="right" alt="Donna Haraway" src="../images/sociologists/haraway.jpg"/>
    <li>
     <a href="http://www.egs.edu/faculty/donna-haraway/bibliography/">
      Bibliography
     </a>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1983]
     <a href="http://www.egs.edu/faculty/donna-haraway/articles/donna-haraway-the-ironic-dream-of-a-common-language-for-women-in-the-integrated-circuit/">
      The Ironic Dream of a Common Language for Women in the Integrated Circuit
     </a>
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      An early version of Haraways influential essay
      <i>
       A Cyborg Manifesto: Science, Technology, and Socialist-Feminism in the Late Twentieth Century.
      </i>
      <br/>
      A cyborg is a cybernetic organism, a creature of science fiction and a creature of social reality. Haraway argues that we have all become cyborgs, mythic hybrids of machine and organism. In recent Western science and politics, the relation between organism and machine has been a border war. Haraways essay is an argument for pleasure in the confusion of boundaries and for responsibility in their construction. A socialist-feminist must pay particular attention to the redesign of cyborgs, i.e., to genetic engineering.
     </span>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1991]
     <a href="http://www.egs.edu/faculty/donna-haraway/articles/donna-haraway-a-cyborg-manifesto/">
      A Cyborg Manifesto: Sicence, Technology, and Socialist-Feminism in the Late Twentieth Century
     </a>
     <br/>
     In:
     <a href="http://www.amazon.com/gp/product/0415903874/ref=as_li_tf_tl?ie=UTF8&amp;camp=1789&amp;creative=9325&amp;creativeASIN=0415903874&amp;linkCode=as2&amp;tag=sociosite-20">
      Simians, Cyborgs, and Women: The Reinvention of Nature
     </a>
     <img alt="" border="0" height="1" src="http://ir-na.amazon-adsystem.com/e/ir?t=sociosite-20&amp;l=as2&amp;o=1&amp;a=0415903874" style="border:none !important; margin:0px !important;" width="1"/>
     (New York: Routledge, 1991), pp. 149-181.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1992]
     <a href="http://www.egs.edu/faculty/donna-haraway/articles/donna-haraway-the-promises-of-monsters-a-regenerative-politics-for-inappropriated-others/">
      The Promise of Monsters: A Regenerative Politics for Inapproriate/d Others
     </a>
     <br/>
     In: Lawrence Grossberg / Cary Nelson / Paula A. Treichler (eds.) [1993] Cultural Studies. New York: Routledge. pp. 295-337.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [1992]
     <a href="http://www.egs.edu/faculty/donna-haraway/articles/donna-haraway-reproducing-the-future/">
      Reproducing the Future: Essays on Anthropology, Jinship and the New Reproductive Technologies
     </a>
     <br/>
     In: Marilyn Strathern [1992] Reproducing the Future. New York:Routledge, p.208.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [2000]
     <a href="http://www.egs.edu/faculty/donna-haraway/articles/birth-of-the-kennel/">
      Birth of the Kennel

    <li>
     [2003]
     <a href="http://www.electronicbookreview.com/thread/technocapitalism/interview">
      Prospects for a Materialist Informatics
     </a>
     <br/>
     An Interview with Donna Haraway by Lisa Nakamura. In: Electronic Book Review. 2003.

    <li>
     [2007]
     <a href="http://tcs.sagepub.com/content/23/7-8/135.full.pdf">
      When Have We Never Been Human, What is to be Done?
     </a>
     <br/>

    </li>

   <b>
    <i>
     Horkheimer Live
    </i>
   </b>
   <ul>
    <li>
     <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=5lmLMFJXuSk&amp;feature=related">
      Zu Marx und zur Kritischen Theorie
     </a>
     [1:52]
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      Translation: This sociology went beyond the critical theory of society conceived by Marx in order to reflect reality more adequately. For, one point is very important: Marx had the ideal of a society of free human beings. He believed that this capitalist society would necessarily have to be [overcome] by the solidarity spelled by the increasing immiseration of the working class. This idea is wrong. This society in which we live does not immiserate the workers but helps them to build a better life.
      <br/>
      And apart from that, Marx did not see that freedom and justice are dialectical concepts. The more freedom, the less justice, and the more justice, the less freedom. The critical theory which I conceived later is based on the idea that one cannot determine what is good, what a good, a free society would look like in the society which we now live in. We lack the [theoretical, philosophical] means. But we can bring up the negative aspects of this society, which we want to change.
     </span>
     <p>
     </p>

     In: Mark Warren (ed.) [1999] Democracy and Trust. New York/ Cambridge: Cambridge Univ. Press, pp. 88-120.
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      Inglehart argues that interpersonal trust is a relatively enduring characteristic of given societies and that it is conducive to stable democracy. However, democratic institutions do not necessarily produce interpersonal trust.
     </span>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     [2005] (with Christian Welzel and Franziska Deutsch)
     <a href="http://www.psc.isr.umich.edu/pubs/abs.html?ID=2963">
      Social capital, voluntary associations and collective action: Which aspects of socail capital have the greatest civic payoff?
     </a>
     <br/>
     In: Journal of Civil Society, 1 (2): 121-46.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=4jr-i60VIdk&amp;feature=related">
      How economic growth, democratization and social tolerance lead to happiness
     </a>
     [5:23]
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      Inglehart says greater economic growth, democratization and social tolerance in 45 of 52 countries for which substantial time series data was available highlights a trend of growing happiness worldwide.
     </span>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://polisci.lsa.umich.edu/faculty/ringlehart.html">
      Home page of R. Inglehart
     </a>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://www.psc.isr.umich.edu/people/profile.html?ID=674">
      Publications of Ronald Ingelhart
     </a>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://www.worldvaluessurvey.org/">
      World Values Survey
     </a>
     <br/>
     A worldwide investigation of sociocultural and political change.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ronald_Inglehart">
      Wikipedia: Ronald_Inglehart
     </a>
    </li>
   </ul>

    <a name="KEARL">
    </a>
   </p>
   <table cellpadding="4" width="100%">
    <tr>
     <td bgcolor="#336699">
      <a href="#top" target="_self">
       <img align="right" alt="Index" border="0" src="../images/up.gif"/>
      </a>
      <font color="WHITE" size="2">
       <b>
        Kearl, Michael C.
       </b>
      </font>
     </td>
    </tr>
   </table>
   <p>
   </p>
   <ul>
    <img align="right" alt="Michael C. Kearl" src="../images/sociologists/kearl_michael.jpg"/>
    <li>
     <a href="http://www.trinity.edu/~mkearl/time.html">
      The Times of Our Lives - Investigations into Socio-chronology
     </a>
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      A sociology of time and social rythms by Michael Kearl (Trinity University, USA). A study of time and the various timetables and rhythms that shape our behaviors and thoughts. The issues are: the different meanings we give to each day of the week &amp; months of the year; the quality time that working parents worry about sharing enough of with their children; the pressure we feel to be on time in the face of dreaded deadlines; the various social clocks whose tickings seem to govern our lives, such as the ages at which we believe we should be married, have children, or be peaking in our careers; the emergence of flexitime and four-day weeks in the world of work; and the types of time that religions impose to fortify the moralities of their members, such as eternity in heavens or hells, purgatory time, or escaping the cycle of death and rebirth.
     </span>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://www.trinity.edu/mkearl/">
      Sociological Tour Through Cyberspace
     </a>
     <br/>
     <span style="line-height:17px;font-size:11px;">
      Michael Kearl  takes you on a trip through Sociology as it exists on the Net. You wont be disappointed. He will show you that the cyberspace medium can truly be a theater of ideas.
     </span>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://www.trinity.edu/~mkearl/death.html">
      Sociology of Death and Dying
     </a>
     <br/>
     An exploration in how death is an indicator of life. Essays, commentary, graphs and links on the death issues of our time: abortion, euthanasia, capital punishment, life expectancies of different groups, genocide and more. Also cross-national study in progress examining why belief in life after death greatest among Americans.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://www.trinity.edu/~mkearl/socpsy.html">
      A Sociological Social Psychology
     </a>
     <br/>
     A sociological perspective written by Michael Kearl. A very good place to start with.
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://www.Trinity.Edu/~mkearl/family.html">
      Marriage &amp; Family Processes
     </a>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://www.Trinity.Edu/~mkearl/geron.html">
      Social Gerontology
     </a>
     <p>
     </p>
    </li>
    <li>
     <a href="http://WWW.Trinity.Edu/~mkearl/strat.html">
      Social Inequality
     </a>
     <p>
